Today, extensive studies are dedicated to the calibration of the normal-reversal patterns in the oceanic crust on one hand and known timescales derived from the dating of basalt layers in sedimentary sequences (<a href="/wiki/Magnetostratigraphy" title="Magnetostratigraphy">magnetostratigraphy</a>) on the other, to arrive at estimates of past spreading rates and plate reconstructions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_refining_of_the_theory">Definition and refining of the theory</span></h3>
<p>After all these considerations, Plate Tectonics (or, as it was initially called "New Global Tectonics") became quickly accepted in the scientific world, and numerous papers followed that defined the concepts:</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1965, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuzo_Wilson" title="Tuzo Wilson">Tuzo Wilson</a> who had been a promotor of the sea floor spreading hypothesis and continental drift from the very beginning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1963_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1963-54">[54]</a></sup> added the concept of <a href="/wiki/Transform_fault" title="Transform fault">transform faults</a> to the model, completing the classes of fault types necessary to make the mobility of the plates on the globe work out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1965_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1965-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>A symposium on continental drift was held at the Royal Society of London in 1965 which must be regarded as the official start of the acceptance of plate tectonics by the scientific community, and which abstracts are issued as <a href="#CITEREFBlacketBullardRuncorn1965">Blacket, Bullard &amp; Runcorn (1965)</a>. In this symposium, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bullard" title="Edward Bullard">Edward Bullard</a> and co-workers showed with a computer calculation how the continents along both sides of the Atlantic would best fit to close the ocean, which became known as the famous "Bullard's Fit".</li>
<li>In 1966 Wilson published the paper that referred to previous plate tectonic reconstructions, introducing the concept of what is now known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilson_Cycle" title="Wilson Cycle">Wilson Cycle</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1966_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1966-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1967, at the <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a>'s meeting, <a href="/wiki/W._Jason_Morgan" title="W. Jason Morgan">W. Jason Morgan</a> proposed that the Earth's surface consists of 12 rigid plates that move relative to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1968_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1968-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two months later, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_Le_Pichon" title="Xavier Le Pichon">Xavier Le Pichon</a> published a complete model based on 6 major plates with their relative motions, which marked the final acceptance by the scientific community of plate tectonics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELe_Pichon1967_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELe_Pichon1967-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Dan_McKenzie_(geophysicist)" title="Dan McKenzie (geophysicist)">McKenzie</a> and Parker independently presented a model similar to Morgan's using translations and rotations on a sphere to define the plate motions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKenzieParker1967_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKenzieParker1967-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Implications_for_biogeography">Implications for biogeography</span></h2>
<p>Continental drift theory helps biogeographers to explain the disjunct <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeographic</a> distribution of present-day life found on different continents but having <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">similar ancestors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMossWilson1998_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMossWilson1998-60">[60]</a></sup> In particular, it explains the Gondwanan distribution of <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">Antarctic flora</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_reconstruction">Plate reconstruction</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plate_reconstruction" title="Plate reconstruction">Plate reconstruction</a></div>
<p>Reconstruction is used to establish past (and future) plate configurations, helping determine the shape and make-up of ancient supercontinents and providing a basis for paleogeography.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_plate_boundaries">Defining plate boundaries</span></h3>
<p>Current plate boundaries are defined by their seismicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECondie1997_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECondie1997-61">[61]</a></sup> Past plate boundaries within existing plates are identified from a variety of evidence, such as the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophiolites" title="Ophiolites">ophiolites</a> that are indicative of vanished oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELliboutry2000_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELliboutry2000-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Past_plate_motions">Past plate motions</span></h3>
<p>Tectonic motion first began around three billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kranendonk2011_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kranendonk2011-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="(December 2014)">why?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Various types of quantitative and semi-quantitative information are available to constrain past plate motions. The geometric fit between continents, such as between west Africa and South America is still an important part of plate reconstruction. Magnetic stripe patterns provide a reliable guide to relative plate motions going back into the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The tracks of hotspots give absolute reconstructions, but these are only available back to the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorsvik2008_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorsvik2008-65">[65]</a></sup> Older reconstructions rely mainly on paleomagnetic pole data, although these only constrain the latitude and rotation, but not the longitude. Combining poles of different ages in a particular plate to produce apparent polar wander paths provides a method for comparing the motions of different plates through time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1992_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1992-66">[66]</a></sup> Additional evidence comes from the distribution of certain <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> types,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> faunal provinces shown by particular fossil groups, and the position of <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogenic belts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorsvik2008_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorsvik2008-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_break-up_of_continents">Formation and break-up of continents</span></h3>
<p>The movement of plates has caused the formation and break-up of continents over time, including occasional formation of a <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> that contains most or all of the continents. The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a> or Nuna formed during a period of <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2000–1800">2,000 to 1,800</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> and broke up about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1500–1300">1,500 to 1,300</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a> is thought to have formed about 1 billion years ago and to have embodied most or all of Earth's continents, and broken up into eight continents around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=600">600</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>. The eight continents later re-assembled into another supercontinent called <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>; Pangaea broke up into <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasia</a> (which became North America and Eurasia) and <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> (which became the remaining continents).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, the world's tallest mountain range, are assumed to have been formed by the collision of two major plates. Before uplift, they were covered by the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_plates">Current plates</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_tectonic_plates" title="List of tectonic plates">List of tectonic plates</a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plate_tectonics_map.gif" title="Plate tectonics map"><img alt="Plate tectonics map" data-file-height="2313" data-file-width="3577" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/500px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/750px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Plate_tectonics_map.gif/1000px-Plate_tectonics_map.gif 2x" width="500"/></a></div>
<p>Depending on how they are defined, there are usually seven or eight "major" plates: African, Antarctic, Eurasian, North American, <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a>, Pacific, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian</a>. The latter is sometimes subdivided into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian</a> plates.</p>
<p>There are dozens of smaller plates, the seven largest of which are the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Plate" title="Arabian Plate">Arabian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Plate" title="Caribbean Plate">Caribbean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Fuca_Plate" title="Juan de Fuca Plate">Juan de Fuca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Plate" title="Cocos Plate">Cocos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Plate" title="Nazca Plate">Nazca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Plate" title="Scotia Plate">Scotia</a>.</p>
<p>The current motion of the tectonic plates is today determined by remote sensing satellite data sets, calibrated with ground station measurements.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_celestial_bodies_.28planets.2C_moons.29">Other celestial bodies (planets, moons)</span></h2>
<p>The appearance of plate tectonics on <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> is related to planetary mass, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">more massive planets than Earth</a> expected to exhibit plate tectonics. Earth may be a borderline case, owing its tectonic activity to abundant water <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValenciaO.27ConnellSasselov2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValenciaO.27ConnellSasselov2007-69">[69]</a></sup> (silica and water form a deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic" title="Eutectic">eutectic</a>.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">Venus</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Venus" title="Geology of Venus">Geology of Venus</a></div>
<p>Venus shows no evidence of active plate tectonics. There is debatable evidence of active tectonics in the planet's distant past; however, events taking place since then (such as the plausible and generally accepted hypothesis that the Venusian lithosphere has thickened greatly over the course of several hundred million years) has made constraining the course of its geologic record difficult. However, the numerous well-preserved <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> have been utilized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dating_methods" title="Dating methods">dating method</a> to approximately date the Venusian surface (since there are thus far no known samples of Venusian rock to be dated by more reliable methods). Dates derived are dominantly in the range <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=500–750">500 to 750</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, although ages of up to <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,200">1,200</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> have been calculated. This research has led to the fairly well accepted hypothesis that Venus has undergone an essentially complete volcanic resurfacing at least once in its distant past, with the last event taking place approximately within the range of estimated surface ages. While the mechanism of such an impressive thermal event remains a debated issue in Venusian geosciences, some scientists are advocates of processes involving plate motion to some extent.</p>
<p>One explanation for Venus's lack of plate tectonics is that on Venus temperatures are too high for significant water to be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKasting1988_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKasting1988-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The Earth's crust is soaked with water, and water plays an important role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Shear_zone" title="Shear zone">shear zones</a>. Plate tectonics requires weak surfaces in the crust along which crustal slices can move, and it may well be that such weakening never took place on Venus because of the absence of water. However, some researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> remain convinced that plate tectonics is or was once active on this planet.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">Mars</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">Geology of Mars</a></div>
<p>Mars is considerably smaller than Earth and Venus, and there is evidence for ice on its surface and in its crust.</p>
<p>In the 1990s, it was proposed that <a href="/wiki/Martian_dichotomy" title="Martian dichotomy">Martian Crustal Dichotomy</a> was created by plate tectonic processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESleep1994_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESleep1994-72">[72]</a></sup> Scientists today disagree, and think that it was created either by upwelling within the Martian <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> that thickened the crust of the Southern Highlands and formed <a href="/wiki/Tharsis" title="Tharsis">Tharsis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhongZuber2001_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhongZuber2001-73">[73]</a></sup> or by a giant impact that excavated the <a href="/wiki/Vastitas_Borealis" title="Vastitas Borealis">Northern Lowlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrews-HannaZuberBanerdt2008_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrews-HannaZuberBanerdt2008-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a> may be a tectonic boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tectonic_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-tectonic-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observations made of the magnetic field of Mars by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a></i> spacecraft in 1999 showed patterns of magnetic striping discovered on this planet. Some scientists interpreted these as requiring plate tectonic processes, such as seafloor spreading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> However, their data fail a "magnetic reversal test", which is used to see if they were formed by flipping polarities of a global magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2000_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2000-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Icy_satellites">Icy satellites</span></h3>
<p>Some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_satellites" title="Galilean satellites">satellites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> have features that may be related to plate-tectonic style deformation, although the materials and specific mechanisms may be different from plate-tectonic activity on Earth. On 8 September 2014, NASA reported finding evidence of plate tectonics on <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, a satellite of Jupiter—the first sign of such geological activity on another world other than Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140908_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140908-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, the largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, was reported to show tectonic activity in images taken by the <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a>, which landed on Titan on January 14, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoderblomTomaskoArchinalBecker2007_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoderblomTomaskoArchinalBecker2007-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoplanets">Exoplanets</span></h3>
<p>On Earth-sized planets, plate tectonics is more likely if there are oceans of water; however, in 2007, two independent teams of researchers came to opposing conclusions about the likelihood of plate tectonics on larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-earth" title="Super-earth">super-earths</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> with one team saying that plate tectonics would be episodic or stagnant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and the other team saying that plate tectonics is very likely on super-earths even if the planet is dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValenciaO.27ConnellSasselov2007_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValenciaO.27ConnellSasselov2007-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
